[h1]"Send Dudes!"[/h1]  HQ: "Nudes...?"  [h2]"NO! I said Dudes! I need HELP!!!"[/h2]  [i]Connection to Kali Carlyle - Head-Engineer, Wube, lost. Transmission interrupted.  Initiating Rescue protocol:  Location - Unknown Status - Unknown Prediction of Survival - Error. Subject is known for relentless tenacity and efficiency.  Error. Rescue protocol impossible.[/i]  Meanwhile in the atmosphere of an unknown Planet  "Landing Vectors's a bust. Landing Gear too... Velocity close to terminal. I'd say... Another happy landing incoming!"   With a loud booming sound the Spaceship cut through the atmosphere of the planet right in front of it like a glowing white hot knife through butter. By a hairs width the engineer managed to soften the crash good enough, to keep the ship at least somewhat together and survived.  Welcome to your new life.  Yes, you! [i]tapping the OLED Panel in front of me with a finger[/i] This is no game. This will become your new reality, if you stumble into it. You love building large bases? Well Yeeehaaaa, you can go as big as your PC can manage without goping up in flames. With the AddOn Space Age you aren't limited to megabases anymore. The numbers are growing. We're talking Gigabases now!  You love efficiency? Good. You can exhaust yourself planing your factory in order to leave this godforsaken Solar System. Oh, Efficiency, what's that? Well then go full spaghetti! Either well, whatever tickles your fancy, it all works. Somehow. In the end... Maybe after a few revisions and ironing out the bottlenecks [i]sharky grin[/i]  Some people call it cracktorio and they're right. You think you completed it? 100%ed it? Pfff, nah. Trophies mean nothing. The factory must grow. And so it will. Whenever you think you are safe from Factorio, it will sneak itself back into your brain. Whispering to you, while you try to enjoy some bedroomfun with your soon to be Ex-Partner... You don't need work... You need to expand the factory. And get rid of the pestering neighbors to do so!  Oh uh... Not those annoying people living next to you, mind you. Biters. Pesky Bugs. BUGS! [b]EWWWW BUGS!!![/b]  They live on your new home. Well... they did. The factory will swallow them soon enough. With related Mods even quite literally. You mine, they sniff it and those eco terrorists will come for you, trying to shut down your wonderfully smoking chimneys and churning smelters!  So set your defenses, equip your Powersuit with a wide selection of gadgets and weapons and go on the warpath. Show those 'Landowners' who really's Boss on Nauvis.  Not enough blood for you? Travel to Gleba and blast the f*ing Hell out of those glebian fivelegged Aliens! Oh... and do it fast. This whole planet is a rotting hell and you'd best be not surprised, when it starts rotting around you. Even IN your factory. Mold, spoiled products, Natives that egg up your defense lines, throwing the next of their generation to the frontlines without remorse!  While you build your factory on Nauvis and defend it, gathering Ores and producing the ♥♥♥♥ out of them to to do research and improve, Gleba will give you pause and force you to rethink. Gone are the days of happy expanding. Spoilage... This dreaded enemy of production...  But maybe the smaller, skittish kind of Aliens aren't your thing? You're a sucker for big game, huh? Well fear not, leave Nauvis and travel to Vulcanus. The game on this planet is... massiv. Like we're not talking big, we're talking on a titanic, lavafountain spewing ginormous tremor and trauma inducing Worms! What did you say? Put a bullet in it's head and it's dead? Heh, feel free to try. I guess Vulcanus is at least a nice and warm grave for the reckless.  On the bright side... IF you manage to evict a Demolisher, how we named those cutsy widdle liddle wormsys, from it's territory, it's gone for good. Yay, you claimed yourself new Land for your Factories! Easy, isn't it? No expanding and resettling from the Natives like on Nauvis or ugh... Gleba...  All this fighting, you want a break? Well say no more... Welcome to Fulgora! The heartattack inducing trashbin of this Solarsystem.  Smelting Ores to produce shiny Bars? Nu'uh! Not on this Holiday Paradise! You will enjoy diving through trash. Literally. Gleba fried your Brain with how to make everything work despite everything rotting down around you? Fulgora demands your best reverseengineering skills from you! Get those heaps of Trash, recycle them, sort them, use them, recycle them again, USE MO- Ahum. My bad. The Madness got me again...  Oh, almost forgot... No enemies. Fulgora is as squeekypeaceful as the buttcheeks of a baby. With timely eruptions every now and then. Well mainly at night. In form of... uh... frying deadly Thunderstorms. But you'll be fiiiiineeee! Trust me. Just think about all the Trash! Give in to your OCD, Clean up this planet! You'll love it.  Problems with Germs? You hate trash and even the thought about touching other peoples garbage gives you the jeebs? Well... Then you might want to visit our Winterwonderland Aquilo. Okay, given you visited the other planets first to grab some vital techs from them, so you can even survive the journey to Aquilo, but after that little technicality? Ice, as far as you can see. At least in form of small Islands. But it has a nice, deep blue Ocean going for it. Ammonia has such a vibrant color...   You'll figure out to get enough warmth around your factorybuildings to stop them from flashfreezing in this subzero environment. We at HQ are sure, you'll make us proud!   So how does it sound? Love it?   Wanna dive straight in, starting the gears of your machinery? Then become a relentless Engineer today!  For those Technerdy types...  Yeah it's 2D but it's beautiful. No Bugs, besides those you can and will kill yourself, a buttload full of different planets to enjoy and almost endless possibilities to scale your factory.  Say what you want, Factorio is the absolute King of Factorybuilders. Doesn't matter how beautiful 3D Games are, they will never beat the artwork of a full spaghettibase in factorio. [h1] I'll leave this positive review here in tribute to Katherine of Sky. [/h1]  8 years ago, I downloaded a pirated copy of Factorio in a computer cafe, and installed and played this game (v0.16) with my Intel Celeron laptop. I literally can't buy the game because I didn't have a bank account. I even sent an email to the developers if they'd agree if I can mail them cash or pay via Gcash (Now, steam accepts our local payment processor.)  Back then, I was utterly lost on what to do right after the old tutorial. I then went to YouTube to find guides and that's where I discovered Katherine's Channel. I downloaded all her videos (Entry Level to Megabase) using a dodgy site called YouTube CC Converter.  I watched all 93 videos, and I played alongside her walkthrough.  I learned about Malls, Balancers, Different Builds, the basic logic of automation, things I could possibly do, everything. Everything I knew about Factorio started from her. 120 hours later, I had a decent sized semi-spaghetti factory that's launching 1 rocket per minute.  Now, every time I play this game, her builds are always integrated into my Factories, no matter how many times I restart, and no matter what mods I use.   She made me love Factorio so much, that I stopped pirating games, and started to buy the games that I really love. With Factorio being the very first game I bought when I got my very first paycheck.  She may be gone from this world, but she will be forever remembered as we build our Factories.  Fly high Katherine of Sky.

Amazing game, if you have a friend who tells you to get this game, then they are not trying to scam you, because this game is amazing. Really love the space age stuff and the amazing mods this game has. The tutorial (first 200 hours of playing) is fun because you explore the game and see how things work, and then game is also amazing after that because you know how the game works and it's still engaging. I would recommend playing this game with a friend or 2, then you can sit and play the game for hours forgetting what the time is. The only part that I don't like is how daunting it feels to play this game alone, so that's why I prefer to play it MP. The alternative gamemodes are fine, the Wave Defence being my favorite, I wish it got an update to make it even more fun to play it. Overall, this game is one of my alltime favorite games I've played. It's up there with the classics I grew up with, and the fact that the game doesn't insist on having amazing graphics is actually a good thing, because that means that the focus is on the gameplay and progression.
I lost 102 hours in less than ten days and I'm close to going back for more without having touched mods, the DLC, or anything of the sort. I have to stop my playtime here before I forget what the sun looks like.   The game is a constant puzzle whose difficulty is determined by your hubris, or your ignorance, and yet each time that loop of Problem -> Troubleshooting -> Solution remains satisfying.
If you enjoy building, problem-solving, optimizing for efficiency- maybe crunching some numbers here and there-, resource management, and varying amounts of pressure from enemies (configurable in settings) then this is the game for you!  This game will probably run on a potato.

I have tried Factorio Demo few times and it never grasped me, until last year.  I started playing Automation games with Satisfactory early 2025, and after reaching the last "stage" on that game, I paused due to overwhelming on requirements, and decided to give Factorio a second chance while my Satisfactory burn out cleared (it never did).  My first issues with the game was the management of the belts, I didn't understand how it worked; is the items per minute referencing one side, both sides, why is it on the left and not on the right, how can I make it go to the other side, or make it fill both sides, etc. I just had to watch a quick video to understand the mechanic and quickly learned how to manage my belts. Afterwards, how to manage the Automated Crafting with Assemblers. I want to build an Electric Mining Drill, and I need ingredientes from both Iron and Copper, I need X of this and Y of that. I decided to craft a long belt for each ingredient but it started growing too much and oftentimes the end of the belt didn't have resources to craft anything. Started capping output chests, moving the mid-ingredients to be crafted on site, only moving the basic ones through the starting belts, etc. Then you unlock liquids, another few hours to learn how they work (thankfully they're easier now it seems, previously the length of pipes affected the liquid rate, but now its just static). You work on your first Oil Refiners, and then start producing new fuel, you unlock train, can transport stuff faster now, then you unlock Robots, that can help you build stuff, then you learn about Blueprinting, easy as Copy Paste, saving them, organizing them on books, splitting by usage, etcetera. It's a never ending cycle of create, find ways to improve efficiency, update, make small adjustments, repeat.  In the end, I was able to finish base game, which is great by itself, then decided to give the DLC a try too on a new save, and was able to finish that too, gave me good 300 hours of gameplay for the money.  I really suggest to try the Demo first and see if it fits your liking; at the beginning I didn't really click with it, but I don't know what changed the second time I tried it out, but it did click. Most probably I changed, and my likings did.

nurch
At first glance, Factorio can look overwhelming — menus everywhere, conveyor belts going in every direction, and enough systems to make your brain buffer for a second. It’s easy to think it might be too complicated or that it won’t quite hit that automation itch. But give it a real chance. Once things start clicking, the game quietly pulls you into a loop of “just one more improvement,” and suddenly hours disappear.  Factorio doesn’t just scratch the automation itch — it demolishes it. Every problem has a smarter solution, every factory can be optimized, and every small upgrade feels ridiculously satisfying. Watching a messy setup slowly evolve into a perfectly flowing machine is pure dopamine.  If you enjoy problem-solving, efficiency, or building systems that grow bigger than you ever planned, this game will hook you hard. Fair warning: you may sit down for 30 minutes and look up to discover it’s somehow tomorrow.

Factorio is one of the most addictive and well-designed automation games I have ever played. The gameplay loop of building, optimizing, and expanding your factory is incredibly satisfying. You start by placing a few machines and belts, and before you know it, several hours have passed and your factory has tripled in size. The famous rule of the game really is true: the factory must grow.  The depth of the systems, the performance even with massive factories, and the freedom in how you design everything make this game amazing. If you enjoy automation, logistics, and solving problems, this game can easily keep you busy for hundreds of hours.  My only complaint is the pricing policy. The game costs around 32 euros, which might be perfectly fine in some countries, but in others it can feel quite expensive. On top of that, the game never goes on sale. I understand the developers’ decision, but occasional discounts would make it easier for more people to try the game.  Honestly, if this game had sales sometimes, it would easily be a 10/10 for me. Until then, it’s a 9/10… because apparently the factory grows, but the discounts don’t.

good
In the grand scheme of things, Factorio is a giant puzzle game. Every challenge in the game can be solved by just connecting two buildings in the correct way, with infinite space and resources at your hand and even zero time pressure if you play in peaceful mode. Once that's done, you might need to add more buildings, so things can progress a little faster. Rinse and repeat. Eventually, you'll find yourself in the middle of a sprawling factory! It does not need to look clean or be perfectly balanced in order to be working - In fact, truly organically grown factories containing a lot of "belt spaghetti" even add to the puzzle element and consequently, to the fun. You can always clean up later, or just move a bit to the left and just build a second factory.  Yes, you can spend a lot of time rebuilding, optimizing, programming your own machinery through circuitry, or add pressure by cranking up the enemies to play it as a brutal tower defense, but all of that is optional. Once you got the hang of the base game, there's a huge amount of complete overhaul and quality of life mods to mold the game into a shape that's more fitting to your playstyle - the official Space Age DLC is basically just one of them, so don't worry about that (or it's pricetag) for now.  The Factorio devs promised the game will never go on sale and stayed true to that for ten years now, so if you want to give it a try, just go ahead. The Factory is waiting to grow!

I gave up at the endgame. I built the rocket silo I thought would mean I had won the game, only to find out I  needed to grind out a bunch more items to get the parts to build the rocket. The thought of hunting down more ingredients, clearing out the bugs, building the same walls, the same train tracks, the same electric poles, the same turrets, the same infrastructure to get the oil from the same oil extraction process was too much. I procrastinated for a bit before just giving up entirely.  This is a sunk cost fallacy game. By the time you realize the tech tree will not reward you with anything interesting you have already spent too many hours already playing, so you just try to reach the endgame to make it worthwhile.  Completing the tech tree is the only objective in the game and it is dull. It's just building basic items to throw into the research building to make the progress bar grow. You don't get tech that seriously helps you make the research items after about the first third of tech tree, but you have to keep making a larger volumes of the same basic research items as your resources deplete and you have to expand further and further outward on the map. If you somehow built the perfect factory, if you took one look at the map and figured out where to put all the conveyor belts and robotic arms and power plants that would maximize efficiency without having to run away later adjusting the outputs, your reward would be just sitting around waiting for the progress bar to go up.

THOSE DAMN ENVIRONMENTALISTS ARE RUINING MY ALL TIME PROFITS
I was initially skeptical of Factorio because the vibe is kind of grim, and before you learn the mechanics it can feel grindy. However, fundamentally, this game is a masterclass in game design. It reveals layer after layer of complexity as you're ready for it, fractally expanding until the point that you've built things you couldn't have conceived of.  Now, I'm predisposed to love Factorio since I love building things and thinking about systems. Factorio is basically "Systems Thinking: The Game". You're presented with an initial very simple system, and you work through how to build and run it. Over and over, you discover new problems and new tools for overcoming problems. By the time you fully understand a problem and it starts to become uninteresting, the game gives you a way to automate it away.  This is all true of the base game, but if you enjoy that then I encourage you to come back after some time away and dig in to the Space Age expansion. I was skeptical how much more they could add, but it deftly adds a ton of new stuff in a way that stays interesting. If you enjoy building and systems and iterating on stuff, then Factorio is a game for you.  My rated time of 300 hours is certainly an over-measurement from having left the game paused in the background overnight a number of times, but it's certainly one of my most-played games of all time, and I still love it. I still haven't finished Space Age, but am at a happy resting point, and one day look forward to coming back and going further.  Oh, and don't sleep on the co-op mode! Building things with other people is fun.

For some reason, yesterday I got the urge to play Factorio again - first time since pre-release days iirc. I downloaded the game, started a default world, and the next thing I remember was coming back to consciousness 5 hours later, at 2 in the morning. Apparently I built a factory in the meantime, and I had a feeling that it was very interesting the whole time. Factorio is a masterclass in catchy gameplay loops, I just don't think that's a good thing. It grabs your attention effortlessly and does nothing with it but eat up your time. What did I spend those 5 hours on? Setting foundations to make a cripple of another pristine planet? That's great. "The factory must grow" (with the implication that everything else is a lesser priority) sounds like one of those classic pieces of satire completely misunderstood as straight forward message by your average linoleum-level IQ gamer, but it is just that in this game. I got sucked in for 5 hours doing an ugly thing, and the game went "based".  Credit where it's due, the visual artists did a great job on this, the Fallout2-esque aesthetics look very good, no notes.

I didn't like the demo the first couple of times but the reviews and my fondness for similar games kept bringing me back questioning my prior decision not to buy.  After taking the plunge and learning how to set up a main bus line to feed my factory, as well as discovering that the combat/defence side of things isn't as overwhelming as I had suspected, I can confidently say that I was wrong to pass on this wonderful game.  The devs are right to not offer a discount as the amount of attention to detail and built in support for mods, on top of an already brilliant game, justifies full price while refusing to deploy the shady discount strategies which plague the industry.

This game is fantastic! I wasn't really expecting much out of this game even after the positive things I've heard about it, but it's so rare to see a game that, while not being completely original, excels at so much at what it tries to do!  When I first played this game, I felt that it was a cookie clicker meets tower defense game, so I was already hooked. What I didn't expect was an almost puzzle game like core gameplay loop introduced into the mix. Basically, you play as an engineer who crash lands on a planet and has to build a rocket to escape. You're given access to very little materials and even less knowledge. You're also being attacked by the enemies in the game called biters and spitters (the tower defense part). You use a bunch of automation machines and setups in order to increase the speed, productivity, and efficiency to reach your goal of launching the rocket (the Cookie Clicker part). This core gameplay loop is actually deeper than that as it can be as simple as putting whatever device wherever it's needed for the time being, but being optimal is the name of the game so it's better to find faster ways of placing machines in your base(s) without solely relying on the number of buildings or speed of individual buildings. It's an interesting setup since on modification to your base can have knock-on effects in ways you may not be prepared for (that's where the puzzle part of it comes into play). The core loop is deceptively simple but you can beat this game if you have enough patience and I find that's inspiring.  What I really appreciate about this games is that you're not going to get it right on the first try. However, that's not expected of you and you get to progress at your own pace. It makes future runs equally as enjoyable as your first run because of this. The game teaches you to be patient with yourself if you're willing to learn and give you many tools to make it easier in a way that doesn't feel patronizing. After all, in this game, just because someone gives you a setup, that doesn't mean you're not responsible for making work nor does it mean it'll work well with everything else. It's also amazing how this game makes you feel so smart for making a single step. If you get stuck and pause because you're confused or need to try to understand how something works better, you're still making progress since you either have machines running in the background still producing stuff, or the situation isn't dire enough to worry you.   There's very little negative I can say about this game. Most of the issues that I have with it is the fact that some upgrades are completely useless and some items are completely outclassed by others in a way that doesn't entirely work for me. I'll spare the details since these are nitpicks for an otherwise amazing product.  Overall, this game is fun and inspring, a rare gem that you don't see too often. Definitely give it a shot.

Honestly, if you haven't played this yet and you're into the automation genre, you're living a shell of the life you could be. It's got it all: moving stuff from one place to another, turning a simple resource into a more advanced one and then some after that, power generation, tech tree/research, combat, upgradable equipment, robots to automate even further, exploration, storage manipulation, logic puzzles with circuits, memorable music, and multiplayer if you're so inclined. One of my favorite things to do is just see how other people solve puzzles with the environment or resource allocation.   The base game and the Space Age DLC are wildly different, and while I did beat the game a few times before it came out, I couldn't see myself ever going back. It compounds upon what you've already learned and then gives you constraints that you have to follow on each different planet. Which a limitation to me just means more creativity in your approach to solving a problem, and I personally enjoy that! [spoiler](Lots of people hate Gleba, but I just think of it more like the closest thing we'll get to a sequel since it is basically normal automation, but every machine requires a specialized fuel instead of just electricity. Between it and Aquilo, it's just another layer of logistics to learn.)[/spoiler]  Even without the DLC, there's a lot going on that makes this a quality game. You get an in-game encyclopedia that gives you virtually all the info you need to know about a tile or entity, a fairly in-depth tutorial for most everything except advanced circuit designs, respawning and corpse-item retrieval if you die, overworld view that you can use to interact with your stuff, programmable alarms hooked up to circuits activating under a certain condition, and it's just fun to watch your factory working. All the pieces coalescing into the end product that you need to win the game. Even after winning, there's infinite research to be done, which prolongs it for as long as you feel. I've had some games go on for 500 hours.  If nothing else, try the demo. It will give you a fairly decent idea if you would like the game or not, and it takes you to science production, which is the crux of progress for the game. If you can't get enough of the original, I feel confident you'll also enjoy Space Age.

[The factory must grow]
After Seeing one of the lads review the game I had to bend the knee, this game is the definition of the factory must grow. Prepare for a few hours of locking in and learning to live and breathe efficiency while battling the hippies that do not like that pollution exists.   Martincitopants got a ton of people to try it and I recommend watching a video or two before just jumping in as its easy and hard at the same time. Trains are chaos, supply lines are headaches. Electricity drives you mad and the impending doom of expansion lurks in all corners. Did I learn how many solar panels I need to power several hundred gigawatts of power to a factory? Yes. Did it take me more than one day to construct and efficiently create the substations and transformers required for the factory to operate 24/7? Yes I also did that. Did that solar panel farm take more than 10 times the space of the starting area after I was done. Indeed it did...  Did the space mod make me go moderately insane when I was grinding almost every day in my off time? Of course. But at the end when I stopped and came back I had no regrets. The knowledge you build up makes every run better. Making you challenge yourself as the Hippies impending doom approaches and war crimes become a requirement, not a suggestion. Prepare and build. AS THE FACTORY MUST GROW

Everything is fiddly and all it takes is one stupid rock or tree in the way to interrupt the construction of the conveyor belt, railway etc.   - It's just as annoying to clear away the hordes of bugs. *Note I have played Factorio before and have hundreds of hours put into it. The point of this purchase was to play multiplayer, but that didn't pan out. According to them, the game made them feel like their head was compressing against itself. Take that as you will.*  [h1] Forbidden Fruit [/h1]  Factorio is *the* automation game. Your goal is to I believe build a spaceship that reaches a certain speed. The DLC adjusts this goal by adding planets to the mix. To reach this goal, you have to build a "factory". What this means is not for the faint of heart if you value your life. You basically have to progressively produce more enhanced materials as you research more and more complex technologies, while also draining the planet of its resources. To do this, you start small, building drills that require fuel and then refineries that refine the raw product into something else (such as iron ore to steel plates). That refined product is then turned into another product in a producer, and so on. It then only gets more complicated from there. Your goal throughout is to automate this entire process, making things easier for you, so you can focus on the other issue: the bugs.  Your factory produces pollution. The inhabitants of the planet "don't like the smell" (even though they choose to build their nests in the pollution), and will attack your base if the pollution reaches them. You can clear them out, but as the game progresses they will get stronger and stronger, and spread out more and more. So if you take TOO long, you may find yourself outgunned by the bugs (very unlikely on unadjusted difficulty). You MAY be a bit frustrated in the beginning, trying to figure out how to fend of the insects, while also keeping a solid flow within your factory (most resource are transported on "belts" until you unlock the robot logistics system). It's a decent challenge, which may cause you to consider going on the attack.  Combat is simple enough, basically just stock up on ammo and hold space to shoot, while avoiding HOMING ACID (I KNOW IT TRACKS YOU. I KNOW IT DOES. DON'T TELL ME IT DOESN'T). But, back to the main draw, you will certainly be engrossed with growing your factory. Eventually you'll expand to getting oil, then uranium, building automated rail lines, etc. You might even decide you need a second base for more iron/copper. Before you know it, 500 hours have passed.   [h1] Pros: [/h1] [list] [*]Yeah, I mean, you don't need me to glaze this game any more than others have. The automation is perfected for those that like those types of games. If you don't, then the game's not for you. If you do, then it is. It's as simple as that. This is a stimulating, engaging game for those interested in flow production/automation.  [*]I really like the variety in the resources that you can produce. Like, it's not just "one" thing that leads to another, rather multiple things can eventually lead to, or be produced from, a resource.  [*]I rarely found myself waiting around for research to finish. There was ALWAYS something to do. Whether it be killing insects, or just expanding the factory.  [*]Soundtrack is haunting, yet relaxing. Interrupted by your factory that is steadily polluting the environment. [*]I'm bad at optimizing things, but the game doesn't really punish you for it, so long that it works. I think it just takes longer.  [/list] [h1] Cons: [/h1] [list] [*]Now onto the biggest con. I strongly believe that the game's addiction factor is a detriment to its enjoyment. Before you laugh at me, many of these reviews have hundreds (or even over a thousand) hours in this game. These bursts of play may genuinely interfere with your life. If you don't care or think this is absurd, then ignore me, but speaking from experience when I first started playing this I had trouble sleeping. It literally becomes a bad habit, where you're constantly thinking about it. I really do feel like those other reviews aren't joking around about losing friends and sleep. [*]There was a point where I stopped playing and came back to it like after a few months, and I just completely forgot what I was doing. This game is so gigantic that if you don't remember the details, you can get lost in your own factory eventually, which is funny. [*]Sometimes there's certain situations where it's like "I can't do anything with this resource right now". And you basically have to make more than you need, or store it. If only there was a way to dump it or something. Like by burning it through a flare in stacks. A flarestack, if you will.  [*]This is more of a mechanic criticism than a game criticism, but you have an ability to detect resources from anywhere. The problem is that you are quite slow in this massive world, even with a vehicle. I mean, technically you can put trains everywhere, but is that what you want to do then like add some sort of plane or jet pack *cough* to the game? [*]Why do I need to make landfills over water tiles when building a rail? [/list]  [b] Verdict: 9/10 [/b]

This review concerns the core Factorio game, and loop, and not the Space Age DLC. I feel that DLC deserves its own review, which doesn't concern the core game. [hr][/hr]  Factorio is the most well-rounded game-game I could have ever hoped for, all without being overwhelming. It features a very simple, core loop; extract resource, create something from that resource, use what you created to extract resources more efficiently. That's the whole loop. Similar to most survival crafting games, inspired by Minecraft. This is all with the initial end goal of launching a rocket to space.  Where it differs is how you apply resources, and develop your game. Amongst the things you can make are machines that can automate the crafting process. That means, through a few simple steps, you go from hand-extracting and crafting resources, to creating automated extraction and crafting tools, to do all the work for you. Where games like Minecraft ask you to stay in the initial loop of digging resources yourself for the entirety of the game, Factorio tells you to view everything around you as an automation problem to be solved.  And that is fun as hell... if you enjoy problem solving as a game.  All of my play time in this game has been in two save files; 150 hours in the core Factorio game, 250 hours (of which I'm not close to done yet) in a Space Age save. And I have to work hard to not fall into playing this game obsessively, becuase it really is that much fun of a time sink.  I can easily avoid all my responsibilities in a day because I decided I needed to fix an item flow somewhere in my base, because I now know better ways to do it. Or I might envision a train cargo system that automatically assigns trains to resource depots, and I've now spent 14 hours straight doing exactly that, by accident.  This is an incredible game, and dangerously fun.  This barely mentions the fantastic art design, brilliant music and sound effects, research trees, unique weapons and items you can create, the constant sense of progression from the natural flow of the game, and the endless ways to approach every problem. It is a sandbox game with unique constraints that makes it endlessly more enjoyable in its own ways.  I always recommend to try the demo first, to see if it's for you, and to introduce the core mechanics. If you like it, get the game itself. It's never on sale, always $30, and always will be (at least at time of writing).  If you finish the core game, launch a rocket, and want a further, nuanced goal, with more challenging ways to play the game, I suggest getting the Space Age DLC too. But again, only AFTER you've finished the core game once.  This is my favourite game ever made, and it feels like it was personally devised for my specific niche. I  highly recommend it to anyone interested, and caution you in the time you're about to spend.  Best of luck, and the factory must grow.

the factory must grow
Probably the most addictive game I've ever played.  The game is designed so that it gives you enough motivation and incentive to follow bread crumbs to progress from nothing into understanding the very basics and later into more advanced concepts. There's always that spark that keeps your brain stimulated to figure out how to use a new piece of technology that was just unlocked, and once you know how one things works, you are always wondering 'if you can then try to do something else based on this new knowledge' and the answer is almost always - YES, YES YOU CAN.   Factorio can be a sandbox game, an exploration game, it certainly focuses on automation but it can also be somewhat of a tower defense game, if you want it to be. Every facet of the game can have customization and logic defined with circuit conditions and I/O triggers.  The game doesn't place a requirement on making use of these and you can easily build a megabase without getting into circuit logic and programming. Once you figure out how these components work and find their usefulness then you realize there's infinite layers to the control and management which can be done and its almost like learning to play the game all over again but with a newfound sense of purpose.   The factory must grow.

Factorio is an absolute masterpiece of depth and design, offering one of the most satisfying crafting and automation systems ever made; every new production line feels like solving a beautifully complex puzzle, and the game constantly rewards experimentation, optimization, and exploration as you scale from humble burner drills to sprawling megabases that span entire continents. Whether you want to play casually and tinker at your own pace or challenge yourself with tricky achievements like "Lazy bastard" or "Keeping your hands clean", the game fully supports your style without ever forcing a single “correct” way to play. World generation is incredibly customizable, letting you tailor resources, enemies, and terrain to your liking, and the modding scene adds near-infinite replayability with total conversions and quality-of-life tweaks alike. On top of that, seamless multiplayer lets you build massive factories with friends (or descend into glorious production-line chaos together), and with the sheer amount of content, progression layers, and optimization depth available, it’s a game you can sink hundreds, if not thousands, of hours into and still feel like you’re just scratching the surface.

The factory must grow.  Each step forward in the game results in a new puzzle. As you solve once puzzle you'll find new ones come out of nowhere. Blue Chips are low? You trace it back to red chips. YOu then find red chip production is low because green chips are low. Why are green chips low? You forgot to expand green chip production. So you finally do that and then realize that you don't have enough copper coming in to fill your new factory.  Every step makes you look back at your factory to fix, remake and solve new issue/puzzles. highly recommend
Hm, I don't like this game, and usually that's enough for me to *not* recommend a game, because why would I recommend something I don't like? But, honestly, Factorio is a pretty brilliant game. If you're the sort that loves working on projects, this game is like crack cocaine for you. The gameplay loop is so tight, it's so easy to just iterate in this game, yet it feels meaningful unlike other infinite iteration games (cookie clicker, most minecraft modpacks, etc). This game is an *easy* recommend for me, because I know that if you're the sort of person who likes this game, you'll *love* it. So it's worth recommending trying this just for the chance that that happens. If you're not the sort of person who loves endless iteration (maybe you get bored of survival minecraft / terraria), or if you find coding an experience of banging your head against the wall, waiting for your code to just work so you can be done with it, skip factorio, you probably won't enjoy it so much.

Amazing and addictive gameplay
Factorio is the undisputed gold standard of the automation genre. What starts with a single furnace ends in a sprawling, automated empire of trains and logistics bots. The "one more fix" loop is incredibly addictive, the optimization is flawless, and the modding scene offers infinite replayability. I’ve introduced dozens of friends to this game and we all agree: it is worth every cent. Buy it, but say goodbye to your free time. 10/10.
This game is so addictive! It's really value for money. I've been playing for > 100  hours and I still didn't complete my first play-through. The game allows you choosing how you want to play, I choose to take it easy and just wait for some research to be completed -- but there are whole communities out there who try to speed-run and optimize everything. Whatever floats your boat I guess. Love this game.

Nothing like it
I don’t usually write reviews, but after almost 1000 hours in Factorio I kinda feel like I have to.  I’ve played both vanilla and heavily modded runs, and somehow the game never stops being interesting. Every time I start a new seed, couple hours later, it turns into “just one more improvement”… and suddenly it’s 3 AM and I’m redesigning an entire production line because one tiny bottleneck bothered me. And then it's 6 AM because you can't leave the temporary fix unoptimized for ups or aesthetics.  What really makes Factorio special, for me, is how it clicks with your brain. You’re always thinking, optimizing, fixing, expanding and when everything finally flows perfectly, it’s insanely satisfying. No other game gives me that same feeling. And all that without the flashy graphics or animations...  And yes, I love pushing it to the limit. I started on a laptop, then upgraded to a 7800X3D, and now I’m on a 9950X3D and I’m still dragging some runs below 60 UPS. Building bases big enough to stress even that hardware is honestly part of the fun.   Mods make it EVEN better. Every big mod pack feels like learning the game all over again in the best way possible (especially SE).  This isn’t just a good game it’s one of those rare games that completely takes over your brain in a good way. Nearly 1000 hours later and I’m still excited to launch another run. Just need free time!  10/10 — the factory must grow.

Just for context, I hate 2D type games, I hate super complicated games, I hate games that are not that easy to understand.   This is the perception that I first had of factorio pre-purchase. BUT WOW WAS I WRONG!!!!  The night that i bought this game I played for 3 hours straight and only stopped because I had work in the morning. I was immediatley sucked into a very beginner friendly tutorial and my mind began to church on how I could make my factory more automated and efficient. This sucked me into the immersive creativity of this game and how you can really make your base HOWEVER you want.   This game so far is worth every penny and I plan on putting in probably hundreds of hours! No lag, Not extremly difficult, not another boring 2D sim, You could EASILY put in hundreds of hours in this game.   Thank you Devs for making a game that is this deep yet FUN at the same time, many devs fail to do this, especially with 2D games. If you like building things to build things that build other things, this game is for you.
Look, I'm going to be honest with you. I am *bad* at this game. Not "oh I'm so humble" bad. Bad bad. My factory looks like a plate of spaghetti that got into a fight with a circuit board and they both lost.  I have 628 hours in this game and I still don't have a megabase. You know what I have? Fourteen different ways to smelt iron. I have redesigned my green circuit production so many times that my biters have started bringing lawn chairs to watch. They don't even attack anymore. They just sit there, fascinated, like "is he really tearing it all down again? He is. He absolutely is."  The thing about Factorio is that it's not really a game. It's a disorder. You sit down at 7pm thinking "I'll just fix this one belt throughput issue" and suddenly birds are chirping and you haven't eaten and your coffee has achieved room temperature and you're drawing ratio calculations on a napkin like some kind of unhinged Beautiful Mind situation.  Blueprints are my favorite thing I'm terrible at. I spend more time in the blueprint editor than I do actually playing. I'm out here crafting the Sistine Chapel of smelter arrays that I will use exactly once before I decide the ratio is wrong and start over. It's like being an architect who keeps demolishing his own buildings because the windows aren't symmetrical enough, except the buildings are made of inserters and nobody asked.  Every time I watch a speedrunner launch a rocket in four hours I feel personally attacked. It takes me four hours to decide which direction my main bus should face. And then I change it.  The developers at Wube are either geniuses or war criminals and I genuinely cannot tell which. They made a game so perfectly optimized that it will consume your entire personality. You will start seeing conveyor belt logistics in your grocery store. You will mentally calculate the throughput of a highway merge lane. You will refer to cooking dinner as "a production chain." Your loved ones will stage an intervention and you will try to explain to them that you just need to fix the oil processing and they will not understand because *nobody understands oil processing, not even the people who understand oil processing.*  10/10 I need help.

Factorio alters your perception of time, and seems to also make you forget to drink, eat, or sleep. "Oops, it's 4am" is not uncommon! There are so many ways to engage with this game, and pretty much everyone's approach is different. Combat (if you enable bugs) adds a nice angle to push you towards putting a base together as opposed to being stuck in design limbo.  The devs have created an absolute masterclass in UI/UX - this is one of those rare games where an awful lot of control is handed to the player. Rarely, do you find you have to turn to mods to allow you to fix that little niggle you might have.  Finally, the modding scene of factorio is legendary. Some players go onto to build bigger and bigger megabases, and some players go on to tackle the many, many expansions and overhaul mods that are out there.  Factorio will rarely, if ever, let you go ;)

Factorio grabbed me from the start and hasn’t let go since. It’s incredibly clean and polished, yet brutally deep when it comes to automation, logistics, and optimization, and every “I’ll just fix this one thing” moment turns into hours in the best possible way. The progression feels spot on: it teaches you what you need, then gives you the freedom to build your own solutions and scale them as far as you want.  On top of that, the overall quality is outstanding: clear UI, great performance, tons of thoughtful details, and those constant “aha” moments that make you want to redesign your factory again and again. If you love engineer-brain problem solving, systems, and that building flow state, this is a must-play.

yes
I should have written this review 400 hours of gameplay ago. This is easily one of the most engaging and rewarding games I have ever played. It starts out small, having you just manage a few mining drills that you need to refuel by hand as they slowly deliver you basic resources, but before you know it you've got a massive factory capable of automatically creating any material you could ever need, spanning all across the map with train networks and automated defenses keeping your precious factory safe from the dangerous enemies.  I think the most genius part of this game is how the technologies all build on each other, and how the core gameplay loop encourages you to keep trying to optimize and improve your factory. This means that you always have many things you can do to improve how much your factory produces or how efficient it is. You can always expand your Science Pack production to research things faster, find a new ore patch or oil field to expand to, improve your factory's defenses or upgrade your furnace array. If you're not careful, it's easy to get caught in a loop of saying "oh, I'll just do this one more thing" for hours on end until you look at the clock and realize you've been playing without interruption for the last 7 hours. But when you get to take a step back and just watch your factory work, it's so incredibly satisfying. You can see a huge, complicated network of transport belts, mining drills, pipes and assembling machines and know that you built everything yourself. You know exactly how all the separate elements come together to create so many unique things, and you still feel like you've only breached the surface of the incredible depths this game has to offer.  All this, and the DLC triples or quadruples the base game's content and length. If you like games that require strategy, experimentation and complex thinking over quick reactions and ability rotations, give Factorio a try. You might not understand the game on your first attempt at a factory, or even your second or third time. But when you finally start to "get it", you won't be able to put it down, and you'll be thinking about the game for every minute you're not playing it.  The Factory Must Grow.

This is the greatest video game I've ever played in my entire life.
It's the definitive factory building game.  Yeah, it's good.
I installed Factorio “just to try it.” That was a mistake. Not because the game is bad—because my mind ascended to a level no human institution was prepared for.  Real life is still real life. I know this because people keep telling me. I just don’t understand why it insists on being so inefficient.  I still live in the same house. Same walls. Same furniture. But now I see things. The couch placement is suboptimal. The fridge is a bottleneck. My family walks randomly instead of following predictable paths. I don’t fix any of this—because I can’t—but I notice, and that’s worse.  My friends think I’m “addicted.” Incorrect. I am enlightened.  They invite me outside. I go. I stand there. I look at the sky. “What now?” I ask. They say, “Just… enjoy it?” Enjoy what? There’s no progression tree. No automation. No belts. Nothing is scaling.  The Intervention (Still Inefficient)  They sit me down. Same circle. Same worried faces.  My mom says, “You play too much.” I nod patiently, like she just told me water is wet. “Yes. Because the factory requires it.”  Dad asks, “Do you even hear us?” I do. They’re just very low-priority alerts.  A friend says, “It’s just a game.” That one hurts. I laugh it off, but inside I’m thinking: You wouldn’t call the sun ‘just a star’.  They ask me to explain why it matters so much. I try. I really do. I talk about ratios. About flow. About how everything finally makes sense when the belts line up. They stare at me like I’m speaking an ancient, forbidden language.  Which I am.  The Doctor, Who Is Not Ready For Me  The doctor asks normal doctor questions.  “How much do you sleep?” I say, “Depends on production demand.”  He pauses. “That’s… not an answer.”  “Neither is real life,” I reply, laughing, because this is obviously funny.  10/10. Best game ever made

Now that someone gave up, their last steps impressed in the dust that covers your crypt. The masses of unwashed clothes and bedsheets taunt you, corpses left by your slaughter of time. Something writhes inside them, your only welcome back.

[h1]A Masterclass in Optimization[/h1]  [b]Bottom Line:[/b] If you value freedom, stability, and well-organized systems, this game is for you.  [hr][/hr]  [h1]The Gameplay Loop[/h1] I strongly recommend starting with the tutorial to understand the base mechanics. Once you have that foundation, hop into Free Play. This is where the game truly opens up.  There is no hand-holding. You are free to build, explore, and solve problems your own way. You figure things out step-by-step as you move along. If you enjoy total agency without arbitrary direction, you will love this.  [h1]The Developer Perspective[/h1] As a software engineer, I have massive respect for how this game is built. [list] [*] [b]Optimization:[/b] This is one of the most performant games I have ever played. It handles massive complexity without dropping frames. [*] [b]Visuals:[/b] I was initially skeptical of the 2D aesthetic, but once you start playing, you realize the design is perfectly functional. It is clean and readable. [*] [b]Stability:[/b] It feels like a codebase where "zero errors" was the standard. [/list]  [h1]Steam Deck[/h1] The controls are extremely intuitive, and the performance is rock solid. It is a perfect portable experience.  [hr][/hr]  [b]Verdict:[/b] Highly recommended for anyone who enjoys sinking hundreds of hours into designing, building, and protecting complex systems. It's just perfect for those type of people. Make sure you have plenty of time to play it, it isn't one of those games you can just drop in and play for like 20 minutes. Well, it's certainly not easy to do that haha.

I can no longer enjoy other factory games.
GOOD
Factorio is a game about gathering resources, crafting items, and then ultimately automating crafting items. There is a minor focus on combat. This simply a mechanic to break up the monotony of crafting and providing a consequence to over expanding. In the end once you have mastered the combat it can easily be disabled as it is more of a nuisance. Mind you the combat is not bad but its not very challenging on its own. This is merely any obstacle to manage with your factory.  The main point of the base game is to automate the crafting of items. You will start by crafting out base materials, the gradually upgrade to more complex items to craft. Science items will need to be crafted to unlock new recipes with the ultimate goal of unlocking the recipe for crafting a rocket to send to space. This is the ultimate goal of the base game.  Now with all of this said I am at 470+ hrs of game play and I just now have starting using the most advanced items (I was previously achievement hunting). Granted I have beaten the game much before now with much less than optimal play. The truest goal of the game for most players is optimizing your factory.  For anyone interested in this game, I highly recommend playing the demo. This will give you access to the tutorial which took me about 12 hours to complete. This itself is almost a full game for free as far as how many hours you will get out of it. If you love the game afterwards I would recommend you buy the game at full price as there are many many more hours of entertainment in the full game. Also the developers have previously mentioned the game will never be on sale.  * As a side note I do not own the DLC and I have not played any mods. There are many more hours of content to enjoy as the modding community is very strong *

went into Factorio expecting a fun automation game. What I didn’t expect was how completely it would take over my way of thinking.  After about 26 hours, I’m still in the early/mid game and constantly saying “just one more improvement” — and suddenly it’s 2 AM. The core loop of building, breaking, optimizing, and rebuilding is incredibly satisfying. Every bottleneck feels like a puzzle, and every fix feels earned.  What really stands out is how deep the systems are without feeling overwhelming. The game teaches you through play, and the sense of progression is fantastic. You start with spaghetti, and slowly turn chaos into something resembling a real factory.  Performance is excellent, the mod support is huge, and the developers clearly care about long-term quality.  If you enjoy optimization, logistics, or games that make you feel smarter the more you play, Factorio is absolutely worth your time.

This is one of the best indie games ever and also my favorite game from 2025.  The automation process is really enjoyable, I frequently play many hours in a row. It feels like there's an infinite way to improve the production chain, while I can experiment with assembly groups, mini-bases, defense systems etc. All the new stuff from the research tree keeps the game fresh, so there's a fun incentive to keep increasing the production output and to automate everything to make every base stronger and cooler.  The tutorials are a bit hard to grasp because some subsystems are just not very intuitive - like trains and signals. It's better to just consider them to be puzzles.  The replay value is good: when I start a new save, I come with my refined experience from previous failures, so I can solve certain problems before they appear. There's a speedrunning aspect to the game and it's nice to observe how others calculate the requirements and build a factory that unlocks the ending really fast.  The survival aspect is more about countering threats with creative defense systems - the alien bugs exist primarily to prevent easy expansion, but they're not like a constant hustle to deal with. The fighting can be mostly automated, but there's always room for strategy to make the offensive skirmishes cheaper to execute.

Factorio's strength is the core gameplay loop, that being juggling several things at once: biters, miners, electricity, you name it. It has computer science written all over it, so if you like problem solving and bringing order to chaos, this game is for you.  One interesting thing I like about this game is the research, you have to research everything useful in this game and constantly make sure you have enough materials in order to research them. Not only do you get to unlock new things, but you get to upgrade your weapons, and defend your base better.  [h3]Space Age[/h3] Some people don't like Space Age, and there are a few okay reasons not to like it (okay reasons, not good lol). One of those being that they changed a lot of the crafting stages, for example modules and spidertrons. To be honest they should have just added new things on top of that so that people playing wouldn't be caught by surprise by this change. However I think the positive outweighs the bad. My favourite part of Space Age so far is the quality modules. I like that you now have the option to put quality modules in pretty much anything and have a chance to spawn new, better quality, items. This is incredibly useful for weapons and ammo, but also for crafting machines and other modules. Yes you can even make higher quality electricity cables and they will reach further in area and distance.   Again I haven't even gone to space yet and I already like Space Age. I think I will easily play 2000 hours of this game by the time I'm done with it (if ever), so I think it is well worth the $100 CAD (including taxes). Played this, Satisfactory and Dyson Sphere Project. This is a great factory sim, as are the other 2. If you're wondering which factory sim to choose, here's a few pointers on the three.  Satisfactory: For people who love asthetics, 3D beauty and letting your imagination run wild with factory pieces that don't serve a purpose other than to beautify it.  Dyson Sphere Project: If you love to explore space and build a star cluster of factories, this is for you - travelling through the star cluster is a must and then there's the universal enemy which you must confront eventually. Manage interstellar logistics and watch your ships warp to distant worlds. In my opinion, an upgraded version of factorio.  Factorio: For people who love to optimize the heck out of a factory, it's not as visually appealing as  the others, however the gameplay is centered on managing throughput and it's a more polished experience than the other two. You can play around with some of the gameplay mechanics (heh) and create a factory so efficient, it'll make the Germans seem like the Spanish! There is a little exploration and making train networks is pretty fun. There's some space exploration in the expansion, however it's all top down view and pretty "flat". Still, its quite addictive with the enemies attacking you as your pollution level rises.

This game is easily my favorite game of all time. It has endless replay value, thousands of mods, and [i]so unbelievably many[/i] quality of life shortcuts and tricks to make this game feel good. The thing that makes Factorio different than the other factory builders out there is that the mechanics never feel like they get in the way of your designs. Blueprints are insanely powerful. You'll have thousands upon thousands of construction bots and logistic bots swarming your base just to make your life easier, letting you focus on the fun bit: [b]Designing factories.[/b]  The list of things you can do is nearly endless. You can design rail lines and train stations to carry cargo from your remote outposts to your main factory. You'll be launching dozens of rockets to load your orbiting spaceship to go to a different planet. You'll be raining death from above by remotely piloting a squad of two dozen robotic spiders that have rapid fire rocket launchers. You'll be carefully setting up a nuclear fission reactor and uranium centrifuges. Then, later, you will set up a nuclear [i]fusion[/i] reactor.  This game is unbelievably fun. And, if you don't like how something works, there's almost certainly a mod that someone has made for it. You can truly go anywhere. There's one mod called Pyanodons that adds thousands of new items, recipes, and mechanics. To fully complete a game of Pyanodons takes people at least a thousand hours of gametime.  Get the game. It doesn't go on sale. It's worth it. You won't regret it.  Well, your sleep schedule might.

Arguably the best game I've ever played, especially now with the Space Age expansion.  It has so many components, and can be easily tailored to your preferred playstyle.  It is a tower-defense, survival craft, sandbox builder, resource management type of game that allows you to apply your own creativity to achieve your desired goals.  You can invest time in creating elaborate train networks or keep it simple with conveyor belts.  You can design everything yourself or borrow blueprints from others.  You can build everything "by hand" or let your robots do the job.  You can make the maps rich with resources or scarce.  You can dramatically increase the quantity of "native citizens" or turn them off entirely.  The game leaves this entirely up to you.  A job VERY well done by the developers!

After more than a year and roughly 800 hours in Factorio, I can say this without hesitation: this game deserves real recognition for what it achieves.  What sets Factorio apart is not just that it’s addictive—it’s that it stays rewarding. Every time I come back, whether after a short break or weeks away, the game still manages to pull me in with the same sense of curiosity and satisfaction. There’s always something to optimize, rethink, rebuild, or improve. The factory is never “done,” and that’s exactly the point.  The core gameplay loop—design, automate, scale, solve—hits a rare balance. It’s intellectually demanding without being frustrating, and complex without being bloated. Problems feel earned. Solutions feel yours. When something finally clicks, the payoff is immense.  What’s especially impressive is how well the game respects the player:  Systems are deep but consistent.  Mechanics behave logically.  Complexity grows at your pace.  Even hundreds of hours in, I still find myself saying, “I could do this better,” and that thought alone fuels entire new playthroughs. Mods, different design philosophies, and self-imposed constraints extend the life of the game far beyond what most titles ever reach.  On top of that, performance and polish are outstanding. The game runs smoothly even at absurd scales, updates are thoughtful, and it’s clear the developers care deeply about the integrity of the experience rather than chasing trends.  In short: Factorio isn’t just a game you play—it’s a system you grow into. If you enjoy problem-solving, optimization, or the quiet satisfaction of building something efficient from chaos, this is one of the best-designed games ever made.  800 hours in, and I’m still not done.

Recommended Playtime: 250+ hours  I’m starting yet another new Factorio playthrough, and at this point I’ve completely lost track of how many factories I’ve built, torn down, rebuilt, optimized, and then rebuilt again because I realized I could squeeze out 3% more efficiency. With over 250 hours played, I can confidently say this is one of the best-designed games I’ve ever touched.  Factorio is deceptively simple at the start: mine resources, automate production, research technology. But it very quickly turns into a deep, endlessly complex logistics puzzle where every solution creates two new problems you want to solve. There’s always a bottleneck somewhere, always a layout that could be cleaner, always a system that technically works but offends you on a spiritual level because you know it could be better.  What really sets Factorio apart is how rewarding it feels to learn. The game doesn’t hold your hand, but it gives you all the tools you need to succeed. When you finally understand ratios, trains, circuit logic, or oil processing, it feels earned. Mistakes aren’t just allowed—they’re part of the process. Every messy early factory becomes the blueprint for a cleaner, smarter one later.  The sense of progression is excellent. You start hand-feeding machines and end up orchestrating massive, self-sustaining production lines that span the map. Watching your factory grow from chaos into something (relatively) elegant is incredibly satisfying. And if you enjoy optimization, planning, and problem-solving, this game will absolutely consume your free time without you realizing it.  Even after hundreds of hours, Factorio never really runs out of things to do. Different map settings, self-imposed challenges, megabases, speedruns, or just trying a “this time I’ll do it right” run keep it fresh. And that’s before even touching mods, which add an absurd amount of replayability on their own.  Factorio isn’t flashy. It doesn’t rely on cutscenes or story beats. What it does have is near-perfect mechanics, rock-solid performance, and an incredibly addictive gameplay loop. If you enjoy games that respect your intelligence and reward patience and planning, this is an easy recommendation.  The factory must grow. And it always will.

Extremely satisfying game to play, I put 10 hours in the demo alone. In the full version you have one straightforward goal: build a spaceship. The rest of the game just comes naturally while playing. You'll encounter new challenges and needs as you keep playing, you'll learn through experience what designs don't really work, what mistakes to avoid and how to better plan your factory ahead of time while you keep growing and improving your production.  It can easily hook you for hours while suppressing basic needs like sleeping and eating, but unlike most addicting games, the pull of this game doesn't come from a billion flashing lights and a neverending stream of things to unlock in a repetitive gameplay loop. Factorio keeps you hooked thanks to the satisfaction you gain by learning and improving your factory's efficiency. It feels more like a hobby than a videogame.

Fantastic Game
I'll start by saying (now hear me out), it took me many years to kick addictions in the past - I've only recently gotten past my RimWorld hit, Diablo 2 is always lingering in my mind (but I'm getting there) and then there's DayZ and Mount&Blade; lets not talk about that indulgence actually...  Then only recently I slipped off the rails again and I'm falling into the hole that is Factorio.  I've never played a game that pushes the buttons in my brain as much as this one! At face level, it's a fairly straight forward game to pick up and begin playing but then as your mind gets into the swing of things you realise "there must be an easier way of doing this", or "surely I could automate this task"... before you know it 2 hours has passed, your belts, railways, mining setups, power stations are all fine tuned to pump out the materials, parts or tech needed to get the next project up to speed.   Then the following two hours is piecing together the following step... By this stage you might have decided it's best to redesign parts of the first step for even greater efficiency, then "what if starting the whole area makes everything run smoother and progress more quickly", etc. etc. - you get it!  Then you realise it's 3am and you have work in a few hours.  100% recommend for removing the static in the mind and focussing on some brain tasks for a few hours!

I could spend hours thinking of things to say about the gameplay of Factorio, but it's been done enough to where you should pretty much know by this point if you're going to enjoy the style of game.  What I will talk about is the execution, and to me it's embodied by this phrase, "of course it does".  Does the game have native linux support? Of course it does.  I want to replace my machine with an upgraded version, does it keep all the items and settings? Of course it does.  I want to replace this belt with an underground one, does it automatically delete the old one and give the items back? Of course it does.  Everything in this game just works exactly how you'd hope it does. It feels incredibly smooth and refined, with every single thing accounted for and handled flawlessly.  I want to turn this belt round a corner while dragging, does the game support that? Of course it does.  These tips might be a little hard to find naturally, is there an easy to access list of tips and hotkeys? Of course there is.  The feeling of using truly great software is that every common problem, every common "is there a way to do that?", every common "it would be nice if there was...", there is already a solution. I think jetbrains IDEs are another case of this, where every utility you could possibly want has already been implemented. In an age of rushed, "good enough" software, stuff like this feels euphoric.

This is not to hate on Factorio, nor Factorio-like games. This is an indictment of my own tastes. In many sandbox construction games, there is something its players called 'lawnbases'. A 'lawnbase' is a base where production structures are slapped down haphazardly around the base, often literally on the lawn, in order to quickly advance to the next technology level.  Factorio is THE LAWNBASE GAME. There's nothing you can build to suggest a place you can lay down your head and rest. There isn't any thought given to human interaction with the production processes, and you don't see a thing to suggest you even exist, like elevated walkways. Rather than being a place you live, the factory is simply a leviathan that you feed to get bigger and bigger. The factory may ensure your survival, but you seem to have to serve it more than it serves you.  This brings a huge sense of disempowerment, where you are pretty much nothing without the factory, and the factory will exist a long time before entropy even acknowledges you are gone. I suppose that is why games like this have a cult following, because you must worship your monstrous creation to win.  Ah sh*t, I think I radicalized myself by accident...THE FACTORY MUST EXPAND. (Run away, save yourself!)

[h3]Good things about Factorio[/h3] - Every aspect of the factory can be expended. - You can [strike]shoot[/strike] greet the local [strike]insects[/strike] environmentalists and ensure they [strike]don’t interfere with “factory must grow”[/strike] are satisfied with factory pollution levels. - Factorio’s blueprints enable easy design organization and can be shared. - This game has endless depth.  - With the Space Age DLC, you can explore 5 more factory environments.  [h3]Less good things about Factorio[/h3] - Too much Factorio can lead to sunlight allergy. - It takes a significant amount of time to learn the basics. [spoiler]For me ~120 hours[/spoiler]

The factory must grow
I really wanted to like this game but it honestly feels clunky and limiting. Coming from Satisfactory I thought oh this'll be awesome, I love the gameplay loop of finding resources, building automated lines, making stuff, and using it to make new stuff, but this is just tedious. It feels artificially limited by basic missing QOL features. A prime example is that resource unloaders do not and will never work on a right angle. You HAVE to set them up to pick up from the back and load from the front. This would make sense if the unloader wasn't a rotating arm...but instead you have to download a mod because the devs couldn't be bothered. The thing that finally killed it for me is electric smelters. They're so far down the resource tree that it's baffling.  You get electric mining almost immediately but have to run smelting off of coal for so long it's stupid. My third rebuild was going to become my 5th rebuild pretty quickly when i had to rip those lines out as soon as i unlocked electric smelting. It's a tedious mess and I'm out. If you are good with organizing around weird arbitrary constraints you'll love this game. If you value your time, don't bother.
A time machine, if this games clicks with you then it will consume you for hours on end.  10/10 Recommended!

10/10 The factory must grow! There's not much that hasn't been said. As far as factory games go there aren't many that can rival Factorio, and none I'd argue that can top it. The levels of optimization the developers have achieved allow for quite frankly some absurdly large factories to exist on hardware that by today's standards are lackluster at best. Meaning almost anyone can boot into this game and have a, hehe, Satisfactory experience. An experience that has been refined to be fairly easily understandable, if a little overwhelming at times. Though that's a quirk of the genre in my opinion. Larger factories result in more choices, results in more time required to consider your options. This can become very overwhelming, but isn't necessarily the fault of Factorio, and is more so the fault of factory builder/optimization games. Overall, I love Factorio and would recommend it to those who love puzzles, design, and have a healthy helping of patience.

I started this game with no prior experience for automation games. In February, I first saw this game as just a short span game that I would pick up for a few weeks and drop. I thought this was going to be a simple game. I thought this game was going to be a game to play with my cousins for a bit.  No. It was not.  I stand here with 700 hours and a infinite possibility for more. The cold, desolate ice of Aquilo. The heat, and oppressive lava of Vulcanus. The crackling and blinding lightning of Fulgora. The drenched, fertile wetlands of Gleba. None of these planetary bodies withstood the factory. A machine designed by me, and constructed by me. It is only a matter of time before motion becomes perpetual.   What can I learn from this clockwork of my own creation? I've spend almost a month in this game and potentially more. This served to be a mind jog, a thinking exercise, a time off from this dopamine saturated world we walk though. Only to walk into a possibly more saturating and addicting one.  I advice to get this game for a calming experience. When you are deep in the factory's hands, remember. It's time off from the world. A way to calm down, a way to unwind. It is your sandbox, with every artificial construct you place was made by you.  It is up to you to pick up your tools, Engineer.

Playtime: 1205 hours | 1205 hours at review | Multiple vanilla playthroughs + modded runs | Biters for challenge, peaceful for chill vibes | Train network obsessive | 100% solo (friends have lives, apparently)  When I started playing Factorio in 2016 until now almost 2026 the core gameplay loop remains as addictive as it was in the beginning. Space Age has taken some ideas and invented around them and it has given the game alot of depth particularly after launching rockets. The game remains an evergreen of good game design with a simple to learn but extremely hard to master as you are going to be constantly thinking about new bottle-necks to solve.   Pros: • Trains are god-tier: Building massive rail networks, tweaking schedules, watching 100+ trains zip resources flawlessly—pure joy. Cargo wagons as buffer storage? Genius hack. • Mods = infinite replay: Krastorio 2, Bob's/Angel's, helper packs like Squeak Through or LTN—vanilla feels fresh forever without breaking balance. • Flexible modes: Biters keep you paranoid and strategic; peaceful mode = relaxing factory zen. Toggle for any mood. • Optimization dopamine: Balancing ratios, circuits, power—every tweak feels like mind-training for real engineering. • Solo perfection: No MP needed—build at your pace, no chaos. (Friends? What friends?)  Cons: • Brutal learning curve: Early spaghetti factories die hard—tutorials help, but trial-and-error rules. Watch the many let's plays. • Late-game UPS lag: Giant bases crawl without tweaks (mods or headless help). • Ultimate time sink: 1205 hours? Blink and it's 3AM again.  Gameplay/Mechanics: Core loop: Gather > Automate > Expand > Defend/Relax > Launch. Belts to trains, smelters to nukes, bots for polish. Mods add wild twists; vanilla shines in pure ratio puzzles and rail empire-building. Biters force smart layouts; peaceful lets trains takeover. Who I'd Recommend To: Solo tinkerers, train sim fans, or anyone craving "just one more belt." Mod lovers especially—perfect for engineers at heart. Skip if micromanagement terrifies you. Value: $30 for a lifetime of play? Steal. 98% positive on Steam for a reason—best ROI ever. 10/10
