[b]Factorio[/b] is hailed as one of the original automation games. The epitome of automation big brain thinking, big factory layouts, and consuming massive quantities. It is difficult to think small when thinking inside or about Factorio. This game is also one of the few where the developers have a hard stance on [b]never participating in a sale of any kind[/b]. After four years of early access, five years on market, and one massive expansive DLC later – Where does the game stand today?  [b][u]The Good[/u]:[/b][list] [*] The artwork in this game is fantastic and holds up at multiple zoom levels, which is technically impressive for the game engine itself. This feature is also vital for quickly glancing at the factory and sussing out what comes next. The artwork is also very unique and there is nothing else on the market quite like it. This lends a lot to the character of the game as looking at the factory in motion is primarily what most players end up doing at one point or another. [*] The game engine runs fairly well despite the eventual immense scale of most factories. [*] Blueprinting layouts can easily be handled in a sandbox environment, then imported into current saves. This not only saves time, but can help facilitate better understanding of layouts and play. The community can also share blueprints for easier new player approaches. [*] Logistical challenges are present and require forethought to some degree. [*] Automation through trains and rail networks is handled well and can be a lot of fun. Trains can also become unplanned defensive mechanisms or errant player deaths.[/list]   [b][u]The Mediocre[/u]:[/b][list] [*] The soundtrack is unobtrusive but unmemorable. Factorio can lend itself well to being something to do while listening to an audio book or lengthy video. [*] Biters, the antagonist aliens in the main game, are intelligent and require thought to deal with. However, they cannot be sent through a maze like a tower defense. Options to deal with them are minimal at best and unexciting at worst. Combat can be too fast at times, leading to the player dying as the Biters out damage player repair kits or player damage output. [*] While logistical challenges are present, they are simply fixed by adding [b]more things[/b] until problems are solved. [*] Fun is relative. Either the Biters are the entertainment or throwing oneself against the myriad of design and layout issues is the entertainment. If neither of these things are entertaining, [i]nothing is entertaining[/i].[/list]  [b][u]The Bad[/u]:[/b][list] [*] The game scales poorly. When starting out, the first challenge is to get automation up and running as that is the soul of the game. This is handled by pouring resources into creating science packs and unlocking technology. This is the core game loop for progression. However, after completing red and green science (pseudo tutorial to science and research) – The next steps become horrendously expensive. Want 10 red and green science pack factories? Using a Factorio calculator, this gives a minimum of 30 stone furnaces, 21 electric mining drills, and 28 assemblers to produce 60 red science and 50 green science per minute. Now for the equivalent 50 blue science packs per minute (the next automation tier). An additional 66 stone furnaces, 39 electric mining drills, new oil processing, and 65 assemblers are needed [b]([i]and most likely new resource patches, trains, and assembling train tracks[/i])[/b]. This is using just tier 1 automation machines. However, tier 2 machines shrink the footprint: 33 steel furnaces and 46 tier 2 assemblers (the drills are already tier 2). None of this addresses throughput and the limitations of the conveyor belts. [*] The above means factory layouts become massive quickly. This would not be such an issue if Factorio had this information displayed in some means the player can figure out. However, it does not. Often times, players utilize outside of game tools and utilities to math out what the factory needs and how to meet the minimum requirements. A simple in game calculator mod disables achievements if those are important to you. This seems like something the developers could have addressed in ten years, but still choose not to for some reason. [*] While trying to figure out massive factory layout requirements, Biters are an ever present threat. Playing on default settings, these little annoyances quickly grow into powerful distractions threatening to end the game. They expand frequently and can grow in strength rapidly. Dealing with them directly only amplifies their strength growth requiring the player to constantly keep ahead in defensive research and funnel an ever increasing amount of resources to keep them at bay. The massive layout requirements demand the player eventually push them back and deal with them, which in turn only amplifies their danger. This is a death spiral for newer players. If a player stays ahead of Biter issues, they become a mere nuisance rather than an engaging element of the game. This begs the question as to why they exist in the first place other than to serve as punishment for new players and an uninteresting engagement for seasoned veterans. [b][i]I have read several threads in which veterans usually shut Biters off eventually due to performance issues in the late game as well.[/i][/b] [*] There are additional issues with automation for balancing resources on belts to handle throughput problems and the insertion tools that put things into the many buildings often fail to have the reach necessary to grab the items. With all this future technology, one would think there might be some sort of automatic means of balancing belt issues. The game even throws the solution to this all with automated logistics bots that haul things everywhere inside the factory. Eventually, players can obsolete the belts altogether which can be a better solution. This can also lead to game performance problems due to the massive number of bots now flying around. Getting a single factory setup for logistic bots also requires a massive layout as well [b]([i]that is 135 T2 assemblers, 133 T2 furnaces, and 146 electric mining drills in case you wonder[/i])[/b]. [*] There are more approachable automation games on the market for the asking price these days. As of writing, Factorio’s base game is $35 USD. Satisfactory is $40 USD. Shapez 1 and it’s DLC are about $15 USD. Shapez 2 is $25 USD. Timberborn is $35 USD. Dyson Sphere program is $20 USD. Captain of Industry is $35 USD. Mindustry is $10 USD. Oddsparks is $30 USD. All these other options often go on sale for less. Factorio may have been unique in 2016, but almost ten years have passed and there are other options out on the market today. [*] Finally, the community can be elitist and toxic. The only person I have ever blocked on Steam in 17 years came from this community. This bares mentioning and please understand that this is not every user you come across. Many of them are wonderful and very helpful. However, there are plenty of new player questions that get asked where a [i]veteran[/i] cannot help but chime in aggressively and accuse the new player of some fabricated social faux pas.[/list]  [u][b]The TL;DR[/b]:[/u] Factorio is the genre staple many games seek to emulate. It absolutely establishes the statement, [i]”The factory must grow”[/i] as echoed by many a Factorio player. The factory itself takes on a role much like an Eldritch God that needs daily appeasement in order to hold back the imperceptible horrors hiding on the fringes our shared reality. Factorio is by no means a bad game. However, the lack of approachable in game information flow, scaling in game technology to keep factory footprints (and game performance) manageable for smooth-brains like myself, and a real gut punch to time investment make this game a questionable investment when so many other choices are on the market ten years later.
If you wanna spend hours in a game, this is it.
perfect game, endless opportunities with mods, only downside is that you have to stop every now and then to eat something or whatever bc apparently you "need to sustain bodily functions" which seems really pointless given how amazing this game is. so yeah, be prepared to start the game, blink twice and notice that 12 hours have passed and your bladder is close to exploding. Love it, 10/10, no notes
[h1] "Would you recommend this game to other players?" Well... [/h1] That's what Steam asks me when I post a review, and for Factorio, as much as it goes against the grain, I'm going to have to say no, unfortunately.  [h2] But what about the positives? It can't be all bad [/h2] That is definitely true. Some standouts: - The amount of QoL features present is something other games in the genre should strive for. - The optimization and developer transparency is second to none in the industry and should be displayed as an example of how you develop software and keep your community informed - It runs flawlessly under Linux without the need for Proton compatibility layers thanks to having a native Linux build  [h2] But we're not here to review software design practices [/h2] Unfortunately, the game is marred by some... questionable pricing and design choices. The elephant in the room first - the pricing. I bought this game back when it was much cheaper (sometime back in 2018), and I'd argue that's a great price for this game given the content on display (about 30-40 hours for a playthrough if you take your time), but looking at it now, with its current price tag, I cannot recommend it. While yes, I may seem a tad hypocritical with my 130 hours in this game, aside from a bit of mucking around in multiplayer, most of those hours consisted of a much more clueless version of me bouncing off this game repeatedly over the years before finally sitting down and the game seriously, only to be beat the base game underwhelmed and go, "wait, that's it?"  And what's in the base game? For a game that advertises itself as a logistics sim, there's little in the way of complex logistics required to actually beat the game. Look at a diagram of a printed circuit board and you've already solved the game's biggest logistical challenge - massing resources and splitting them off where they need to go. I had hoped that maybe the mathematical side of the game could change things, but no, it's all stuff you could solve in 5 minutes with algebra you'd learn in secondary school. There's an entire digital logic system you can play around with, but aside from one optional case (which I found quite fun, granted), it has next to no practical use when it comes to your main goal of launching a rocket. So how do we make an easy game harder?  [h2] Solution: grab a hammer [/h2] Every hour - or every half hour if you're unlucky, the fine residents of the planet will decide to waltz up to your buildings with an assortment of instruments of deconstruction and subject you to forced rapid disassembly, of course!  Jests aside, the biters feel like an impedance and an afterthought to pad out a simple game with more uninteresting busywork: clear their nests with a shallow combat system whose automation comes too late, mindlessly build defences by clicking and dragging straight lines of belts full of items whose recipes are so simple you can just mindlessly plop down factories making them without second thought, or just turn them off and live a happier life without their constant tantrums...  [h2] Is what I would say, if... [/h2] The game weren't designed around them existing. There exists an entire research tree dedicated to killing them better, and there's an entire crafting tab dedicated to making the gear that kills them faster.  [h2] But what about mods? [/h2] Admittedly, I didn't touch mods, and truthfully, they shouldn't be taken into consideration when reviewing a standalone game such as this. This game is not advertised as a modding platform, and thus it should be judged for what it provides. Mods may eclipse the quality of the base game, yes, but they don't render any verdict on the base game's quality moot.  [h1] Verdict [/h1] It's a great piece of software, but as a game, it stands marred with game design choices that are either frustratingly simple or frustratingly inconvenient.  Spend your money elsewhere.

The gold standard for factory games.  Most of the challenge is in the 2D nature of the game. Other than short underground connections, you can't layer your logistics design. As more items join the production chain your base can quickly turn into spaghetti with conveyers everywhere.  What's most amazing is that when you win the game after 40 hours you'll feel compelled to play again and try to build a cleaner designed base. There's also plenty of challenging achievements for long time players.  If you're new to factory games, consider this a solid recommendation to start here.
worth the pricetag. but it's a fairly steep learning curve so don't expect to hop in and find it easy.  Though if you do like a game which requires a bit more thinking than clicking it's very rewarding

A prime example of gaming done right. At first glance, it seems simple. A factory building game where you are attempting to automate production of materials, which you will use to produce further items or conduct research. But the genius behind the implementation is just how many ways you can perform that task. Excellent player expression, you are given a blank slate to do with as you please. A single prompt to start the game, you are here on an unknown planet. You play, build and explore. The whole time you feel like you are discovering something new and interesting. The base game of just building a rocket is already a great taste of what the game has to offer. But then space age, my goodness, it takes it up to 11. You are not just building a rocket anymore, you are traveling the stars, settling strange new planets and learning unique and challenging new puzzles on each one.   Then you realize the game has an amazing mod framework, with well documented API. If space age takes the base game to 11, mods takes it to 69. I have never seen a game which supports its modding community like this. Easily install, update, and manage your mods from inside the game. Easily swap between mod sets for different save files, with convenient buttons for syncing which mods are enabled as well as their options to reflect the settings for that save. Then outside the game, they have an entire database documenting everything related to the mod framework and its prototypes. If you want to modify something, its as easy as looking it up and doing it! (not to mention the developer keybindings you can use in-game as well)

I've played over 500h (seems that playing offline does not register game hours). And I can say, I will play at least another 1000h in the next 1 or 2 years... Mi forecasts honestly can't process how many total hours I could play this, if I had time.  That said... I'm addicted, it's one of the best games i've ever played, it's so fun to solve constant issues, curse at my past self because of the poor decisions and then get drones and destroy everything 20 times until I'm happy with the factory expansion rate... Because, THE FACTORY MUST GROW.  I do recommend couple of things for your experience to actually be enjoyable and not abandon in the first 40h. - Before playing your first game, search for nothing, don't open any wikis, you've probably already seen people play if your buying the game. And if not, even better.  - Look carefully at the start screen (you can see interesting ways of building stuff or ideas for issues you're encountering) - Play ALL THE TUTORIALS, you can play chill, there's no rush, simply understand how the game works, it's tops 6-8h of gameplay.  Now you can start a new game. Don't edit anything on you map, just hop straight in. Whit what you've learned on the brilliantly made tutorials, you will be more than capable of playing your first 40h, then, you hopefully have f up enough times to handle the rest just fine.  And now you can open wikis (outside the game, there's's an in-game wiki, very useful to understand basic concepts of the different items) investigate the most efficient ways of doing whatever. I personally have not used a single guide, or wiki other than to understand the red and green wire (now all my railroad system is fully automated, emergency resources for fixing bases, under demand resources, and after 20h with no accidents or jams as well).  Simply enjoy the game and don't obsess on if you are doing things right. If it works, it's good. With time, your brain simply starts (trauma based response) to be more thoughtful with your future self and think of possible solutions to problems you can see will appear when building your whatever.  Buy it, enjoy it, suffer with it. It's like life, but here you actually control something.  Lastly, I do recommend using something like trello once more experienced in the game, that way you can plan ahead and if you ever have to stop playing, you can hop back in and know exactly what you where doing, and what reasons are behind you weird decisions.

Factorio isn’t just a game — it’s an experience that rewires how you think about problem-solving. From the very first conveyor belt, you’re hooked into a loop of creativity, efficiency, and discovery. What starts as a simple task of mining iron quickly evolves into a sprawling, beautifully complex network of machines, trains, and logistics that feels alive.  What makes Factorio extraordinary:  - Infinite Replayability: Every run feels different. Whether you’re optimizing for speed, scale, or elegance, the game constantly challenges you to improve. - Perfect Balance of Depth and Accessibility: It’s easy to start, but endlessly deep. You’ll learn something new every time you play. - Brilliant Game Balance: The pacing of research, resource scarcity, and enemy pressure is tuned so well that it keeps you engaged without ever feeling unfair. You’re always nudged forward, never forced. - Community & Mods: The modding scene and player community elevate the game even further, offering endless ways to expand your factory. - Satisfaction Like No Other: Few games deliver the same rush as watching your carefully designed system hum in perfect harmony.  This is a game that respects your intelligence, rewards your persistence, and sparks your imagination. It’s not just about building factories — it’s about building dreams of efficiency. If you love creativity, strategy, and the joy of seeing chaos turn into order, Factorio is simply unmatched.  ⭐ 10/10 — One of the greatest games ever made.

Kiss goodbye to your life. After the first 100 hours everything in your life will be about Factorio. Getting on a train on morning commute? You will think about platforms being congested and how inefficient it is... run out of shampoo in one bathroom and want to pop into the second bathroom to grab it? you'll realise you cannot take resources out of one system to feed another as you won't be solving the deficit...
Factorio doesn’t just ask you to solve problems - it teaches your brain to crave them. You start by automating a tiny task, and suddenly it’s 3 a.m., your factory is a beautiful spaghetti monster, and you’re optimizing belts like your life depends on it.  Every system clicks together perfectly, the learning curve feels rewarding instead of punishing, and the sense of progression is unmatched. There’s no filler, no hand-holding - just pure, elegant design that respects your intelligence.  Warning: buying Factorio may permanently alter how you think about efficiency. 10/10, the factory must grow.

It is a story about transcendence from Man to God.  You land on a hostile planet, and minutes you have pierced the very heart of the earth, poisoned her lungs, chopped down her trees, murdered her offsprings. For what? For the everconsuming lust, and greed. And within months if not weeks you are floating within your walls,  your fortress, your kingdom of fire and steel. You have hundrends of loyal servants, whose numbers double each day, for such is the nature of your lust. You can tell you are not welcome to walk the surface. Yet you persist. Your dominations on this world grows boring, and you seek to expand to other worlds, for they are rightfully yours.

Favorite game of all time

Great game, I love the automation and the exploration of new worlds with new mechanics. Absolutely loved lava world, but was not a big fan of scrap land. Spoilers follow.  The biggest flaw in the game is the lack of infinite resources combined with the endless attacks from bugs on the home world. So you set things up with a complex network of drones to block narrow passages and prevent bugs from destroying your base while you're gone. If you have holes it's not hard to fix remotely with remotely-operated tanks and building. But eventually you will run out of resources, most likely at a time when you're trying to build another automated transport space ship to ferry resources. Because of distance and the lack of teleporters, it becomes increasingly inconvenient to ferry resources back to your main base while running ammo out to the fringes. So you end up with increasingly large and complex logistics just to maintain, all while trying to expand on other planets.  Again, I love the game. I appreciate that it's still maintained, too. I just wish they'd made it in such a way that you could stop micromanaging mechanics (like the bugs) once you'd mastered them. I'd have introduced something like a large-radius laser turret that actually did big damage with resources from another world so that  the bugs could be wiped out while making it do next to no damage to creatures from other worlds.  We wouldn't have games like Satisfactory without Factorio, and I really appreciate that they realized making 600 parts derived from iron and copper is actually pretty fun for some people.
